B000002U82|5.0|Aside from its reputation as being one of rock music's most successful albums, and its remarkable stay on the Billboard Top 100 charts for an  unprecedented and undefeated 741 weeks, Pink Floyd's Dark Side of the Moon  is artistically speaking, a striking example of rock at its freshest and  most innovative. Although many of the aural techniques utilized on the  album can trace their origins back to many of their predecessors, these  techniques combined with the prevailing themes of the album produced a  compositional masterpiece that was strikingly distinct and unique, placing  it aside from many of the albums of its day. The album which deals with the  fleetingness of life, political disenchantment, violence and madness begins  brilliantly with a faint heartbeat which eventually becomes more prominent.  The heartbeat comes into full fruitation when the ticking of clocks and the  ringing of cash registers comes into the soundscape with sound bytes of  various people discussing their personal views on madness. The point that  Roger Waters (principal songwriter and leader of the band) is laying forth  becomes clear. Waters cites the fleetingness of the human experience and  mankind's propensity towards madness and destruction (hardly the typical  themes associated with rock music). The sounds reach a crescendo, bordering  on the lines of disorientation and confusion and then is broken down by a  series of screams which lead into the first song. The first track Breathe  begins languidly and is accompanied by Gavid Gilmour's spacey and fluid  slide guitar. The song introduces Gilmour's raspy, yet soothing voice which  blends with the mellow feel of the song. The chord progressions are  relatively simple yet stunningly effective. No time is wasted as Breathe is  connected to the next piece On the Run. Although On the Run is primarily an  experimental sound piece, it remains well structured, lush, and textured.  The next song Time, which is quite possibly the best song on the record,  features Pink Floyd at the top of their game. It is at this point that Pink  Floyd brilliantly employs the use of a choir to add to the already stunning  textures of the album. The album travels through the funky, yet timeless  Money all the way to the sublime and tranquil Us and Them. The album  plunges itself into the total decay of madness with the ever brilliant  Brain Damage which is then brought home by the stunning and powerful  Eclipse. The album then fades into the darkness with the same heartbeat  that introduces it. The only minor flaw that latches on to this album is  the instumental piece The Great Gig in The Sky. This piece should have been  shortened with or dispensed with as it is lengthy and mundane and doesn't  provide an acceptable introduction to the next song Money. Despite this  minor quibble Dark Side of the Moon is an evocative and telling concept  album.

B000001GQB|5.0|For Mozart's late symphonies, Bhm is the one who does it best. There really need be no argument about it, because the only people who prefer,  say, Marriner, do so because of the greater tempo and the lightness. THAT  style (faster, lighter, springier, from a small ensemble, like the ASMF) is  more suited for the early symphonies, and I think many people will agree  with me about this.<br />For symphonies like the master 40th, it is not  springiness we want: we want the depth, and the joy-in-tears, which only  Bhm brings out. Mozart had advanced from the 25th to the 40th - the  recommended tempo and style is simply not the same. This happens because he  wrote so many symphonies, something which you will not find in, say,  Brahms. There was a word used by the Amazon reviewer which sticks:  &quot;love&quot;. He said Bhm conducts Mozart with true love and feeling.  Yes, that is definitely it - you can see the conductor's love for the  composer and the composition. And the resulting feeling - is from out of  this world. The ASMF-Marriner simply does not achieve this. We can have  raging debates about whether Mozart &quot;intended&quot; it this way or  that, but I think the concensus is that Bhm's style is IT.<br />This is a  deeply felt, personal recommendation from me. If you want to listen to the  late Mozart symphonies, listen to Bhm.  You will find nothing better.<br />I  have one major beef though. The fourth movement of the 40th is the  shortened version (no repeat of the first theme). Can anyone tell me of a  recording in which Bhm did the full version?<br />All said and done, this set  is IT. Look no firther. Take it from a listener with a lifetime of  experience with different conductors/composers/orchestra combinations.
0671510991|5.0|Diplomacy, by Dr. Henry A. Kissinger, is a comprehensive review of the theory and practice of statesmanship narrated by the most accomplished diplomat of our time.  From the raison d'etat of Cardinal Richelieu to the glasnost of Mikhail Gorbachev, Kissinger describes each era's key practicioners of the diplomatic arts with a familiarity borne of a lifetime of dedicated research.<br />Modern diplomacy is the result of the efforts of those who developed the art over the centuries.  The great statesmen of Europe: Richelieu, Metternich, Bismarck, et. al. each developed their theories of international relations based on their nation's unique set of historical and geopolitical circumstances.  The striking thing is how little things have changed. Kissinger shows how modern statesmen still make decisions based on the same factors today.<br />Dr. Kissinger begins his review of America's contributions to statesmanship by examining our two foremost foreign policy theorists: Theodore Roosevelt and Woodrow Wilson.  Kissinger depicts these gentlemen as the architects of our foreign policy and shows how succeeding administrations have followed the policies of one or the other.  He also reviews World Wars I and II and the dreams of overly idealistic Americans that motivated those generations to make tremendous sacrifices in order to make the world a better place to live.  In both cases, our hopes of creating a world composed of truly democratic nations was dashed by the realpolitik of our adversaries. Still, despite two world wars, Korea and Vietnam, our idealism is intact enabling us to remain a beacon of freedom for all the world.<br />Dr. Kissinger writes about diplomatic leaders, past and present, with complete familiarity.  It's almost as if he's describing a group of close friends that he meets at the club every week for a game of high-stakes poker.  The difference is Kissinger knows them so well he can accurately predict what they will do and why.  We're fortunate he is onour side!<br />Diplomacy is an important addition to the library of every student and practicioner of the international political process.  Advanced students of World History and International Relations will profit from his conclusions.  Beginning students will be inspired to conduct more dilligent research on the events he describes.  I am personally much better informed for having read this book.  You will be as well!

B0000024SI|5.0|Arguably the single most important record released in the 60s, even moreso than that other cultural icon SGT PEPPER - although my personal opinion is divided on the matter (I lean more toward The Beatles on these two albums,  though overall The Beatles and Dylan are equal for their body of work in  the 1960s.).<br />You cannot discuss Bob Dylan without his only artistic  contemporaries, The Beatles.  Both started in relatively the same time, but  Dylan sung fold and The Beatles bubble-gum pop.  In this manner, Dylan has  a much more satisfying early start than does The Beatles.  You don't have  to wade thru a pop phase like with The Beatles.  But The Beatles, of  course, are the single most important band in rock'n'roll, and Bob Dylan  the single most important artist other than The Beatles.   <br />That being  said, Dylan began rocking in a much more satisfying manner than when The  Beatles began.  Their influence over each other is amazing, and because of  this both owe a great debt to one another.  Dylan got The Beatles doing  much more interesting things than the bubble-gum pop of &quot;I Want to  Hold Your Hand,&quot; and their first four albums.  On Help! they had three  or four songs more introverted.  Then, the full-fledged effect of Dylan  over them became apparent when they released RUBBER SOUL in December 3  (they also released the single Day Tripper/We Can Work It Out the same  day). Yet the effects of The Beatles, who encouraged Dylan to move beyond  folk into rock'n'roll on this album, which is his first full-fledged rock  album.  (Some will say that title belongs to BRINGING IT ALL BACK HOME, but  that is only half - it straddles his folk and rock, whilst this falls into  the rock).  This album Bob Dylan released August 30, 1965 - and without The  Beatles this might not have happened.  Both of them show their influences  on the other within a mere four months (although both HELP! and BRINGING IT  ALL BACK HOME points to the direction both are moving,though Dylan's  moreso.  Dylan's record beats HELP! in quality by a long shot, although the  two or three best songs on HELP! give the Dylan songs a run for their money  - and that is primarily because they are like Dylan.) <br />As for his voice,  it is one of those things that it actually is a part of his quality that  Bob Dylan can't sing at all.  That is one of the things that adds to his  charm and importance, because it proved that a singer with an  unconventional voice can make it.  Dylan obviously gave no import to music  theory, and with this further defiance he helped pave the way for other  singers who have good voices, though not in a technical sense.  (Really,  who could sing &quot;Like a Rolling Stone&quot; or his smash single  &quot;Positively Fourth Strength&quot;?)  Its like a Faulkner novel - it  just wouldn't be what it was without Faulkner's style - in this case,  Dylan's voice.<br />The songs are absolutely fantastic.  The ride starts on  the epic &quot;Like a Rolling Stone&quot;, the first six minute single to  hit number one, breaking the code of three minute songs.  In length, this  would be the necessary forerunner to Don McClean's number one hit  &quot;American Pie&quot;, which have numerous references to Dylan (most  notable when the jester takes the crown, and he sings with a voice that  comes from me and you - Dylan's voice is notorious).  Then it travels onto  &quot;Tombstone Blues&quot;, flat-out garage rock, &quot;It Takes A Lot to  Laugh, It Takes a Train to Cry&quot; bluesy material.  &quot;Like Tomb  Thumb's Blues&quot; and &quot;Queen Jane Approximately&quot; absolutely  brilliant songs.  &quot;Ballad of a Thin Man&quot; is one of my favorite  Dylan songs (it earning an honourable mention in &quot;Yer Blues&quot; off  THE WHITE ALBUM).  The two weakest songs, in my opinion, are the title  track and &quot;From a Buick Six&quot;.  They seem forgetable to me, at  least Buick does.  &quot;Highway 61 Revisted&quot; is, like &quot;Tombstone  Blues&quot;, flat-out garage rock, but is not as good.  But it does  contribute to the overall feel to the album.<br />The one song I have left out  would be the last - Desolation Row.  HIGHWAY 61 REVISTED begins with an  epic and ends likewise.  An eleven minute closer that is folk rock, a  surrealistic poem with nonsense imagery, and an incredibly addicting tune.   <br />&quot;Einstein, disguised as Robin Hood -- With his memories in a trunk  -- Passed this way an hour ago -- With his friend, a jealous monk -- He  looked so immaculately frightful -- As he bummed a cigarette -- Then he  went off sniffing drainpipes -- And reciting the alphabet -- Now you would  not think to look at him -- But he was famous long ago -- For playing the  electric violin -- On Desolation Row&quot;<br />This song will be with me for  a very long time.  It blows anything that Morrison wrote away.  The sense  of rhythm Bob Dylan carries with this song is incredible, allowing his  outstanding song-writing abilities to shine.<br />If you want to discover the  history of our culture during the sixties, this is one record you need to  buy, along with SGT PEPPER and several others.  Excellent music.

I also recognised Michael Stipe's definitive vocals from &quot;Out of  Time&quot;. About a year later, having become a fan of R.E.M. after  listening to one album (&quot;Out of Time&quot;), I was trying to decide  what to buy next. Whatever it was also had to be on cassette, because I  didn't have a portable CD player (still don't). At the back of my mind,  whenever I was in a music store, was to look out for the name  &quot;Nightswimming&quot; on the back of any R.E.M. CD I looked at. When I  spotted it on the back of &quot;Automatic For The People&quot;, my heard  leapt, and I immediately searched for the cassette version, and by God,  there it was, amongst &quot;Out of Time&quot; and &quot;Up&quot;, the only  other R.E.M. albums I've seen on tape so far. &quot;Drive&quot; is a  wonderful start to the album, I remember liking it when I first heard it. I  can't say I found &quot;Try Not To Breathe&quot; as enjoyable the first  time around, but then that could be said about my opinion of several songs  at that time. I greatly enjoyed &quot;The Sidewinder Sleeps Tonite&quot;,  and after I got hold of the lyrics, I decided it had my favourite verse of  a song of all time in it (the &quot;instant soup&quot; one). I'd heard  &quot;Everybody Hurts&quot; quite a few times on the radio, enough to enjoy  it while it was on, but amazingly I hadn't realised until now that it was  R.E.M. who wrote and sang it! Whilst &quot;Everybody Hurts&quot; is a great  song (although I can imagine some people finding it irritating if it was  played too often, as is the way with music), I still don't believe it is  the best song on the album. By now, things were going well, I'd had three  brilliant songs and one okayish. Next came &quot;New Orleans Instrumental  No.1&quot;, which I've heard described as not as good as the other songs,  but I can't find anything wrong with it. It was a short little piece with  enough variations of a theme to keep you interested the whole way through.  Following that was &quot;Sweetness Follows&quot;, a lovely and peaceful  melody which I rewound straight after listening to it. The second side of  the tape was where it all started going wrong until  &quot;Nightswimming&quot;. I found &quot;Monty Got A Raw Deal&quot; fine,  but then my mind started wondering when listening to &quot;Ignoreland&quot;  and &quot;Star Me Kitten&quot;, a sure sign that they couldn't hold my  attention. I quickly focussed when &quot;Man On The Moon&quot; started,  because one of my friends told me it was his favourite R.E.M. songs. To be  frank, I was disappointed. I don't know what I expected, but it just didn't  seem to strike a chord with me the way &quot;Sweetness Follows&quot; and  &quot;The Sidewinder Sleeps Tonite&quot; did. Then came the time for  &quot;Nightswimming&quot;, and I remember my heart thumping before it  started, as I still wasn't sure if it was the one I'd heard on the radio  more than a year ago. Fortunately it was the right one, and to this day I  still think it's one of my favourite songs ever. I suppose I have a soft  spot for the piano when it comes to music, as &quot;Tongue&quot; from the  next album &quot;Monster&quot; is one of my favourites of that record, and  it also uses the piano. After &quot;Nightswimming&quot; came &quot;Find The  River&quot;, and again, I lost attention, and didn't find it very exciting.  Of course, I disciplined myself to listen to the album a second time, and  found myself enjoying the songs on the second side much more. I think that  is what happens with a lot of people who listen to this album. Anybody who  hasn't gotit, I advise you to, and if you're slightly disappointed the  first time round, please please please try again, because you're really  missing out. It's worth buying if only for &quot;Nightswimming&quot; in my  view.

The book will definitely  make me think differently about migrant workers that come into our area.  Things may not be quite as bad now as back then, but they probably face  many of the same circumstances.
